يرفعه، ولو طرأ نافذ من أحد الجانبين على نافذ جانبه يرفعه بيانه رجل وكل رجلاً بأن يزوجه امرأة بألف فزوجها إياه على خمسين ديناراً بإذنها أو بغير إذنها ثم زوجها بألف ينفسخ الأول، ولو زوجها الوكيل إياه بألف درهم بغير إذنها ثم زوجها إياه بخمسين بغير إذنها يبقي الأول، فإن إجازته جاز ويبطل الثاني لأن الأول
The person who contracts, even if the contract issued from him, as in al-Tajnis. And ratification of the fuduli's marriage is established by speech and action: by speech, such as 'I ratify' and similar, also 'well done', 'may Allah bless us', 'excellent', 'you are correct', and 'divorce her', except when the master says to his slave as will come in its chapter. By action, such as accepting the dowry, unlike accepting a gift. And her saying 'I do not like this dowry' is not a rejection, so she may ratify. Among the rulings of the fuduli is that he has the right to rescind his contract in some cases but not others, as mentioned by the authors of fatwas. In al-Zahiriyya: The fuduli in the chapter of marriage does not have the right to retract before ratification, whereas the agent in a suspended marriage has the right to retract by word or deed. Explanation: A man appointed an agent to marry him a woman, and the agent married him a woman who had reached puberty without her permission, or her father married her, and the news did not reach her until the agent revoked the marriage by word or deed, such as by marrying him her sister, it is valid, even if he was a fuduli. In the same scenario, he does not have the right [to retract]. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf in his first opinion that the fuduli also has the right to retract. The fuduli in the chapter of sale has the right to retract by consensus because retraction is a flight from liability in the case of sale, unlike marriage. In one aspect, the agent has the right to rescind by word but not by deed: if he is appointed to marry a specific woman, and he married her without her consent, the agent has the right to revoke by word because he is an agent in that, but not by deed, so if he marries him her sister, it does not revoke the first marriage because he is a fuduli in the second marriage. In another aspect, he has the right to rescind by deed but not by word: if an agent is appointed to marry, and he ratifies a marriage he had previously conducted, it is valid by istihsan, and he does not have the right to retract this marriage by word because he was a fuduli at the time of contracting, but he has the right to retract by deed by marrying him her sister without her consent because he is an agent in the second contract. The summary: any contract issued by a fuduli in marriage cannot be revoked by him word or deed because he has no liability to escape from, except if he becomes an agent afterwards, then he can revoke it by deed due to the necessity of fulfilling what he was appointed for. The agent in the suspended marriage has the right to rescind even though he also has no liability, in order to realize the principal's intention, because his goal was not achieved by the suspended contract. So the agent may move from it to others. He is not permitted to rescind by deed in the second case because the principal appointed him to marry a specific woman, so once he married her, his agency ended, so he does not have the authority to make another marriage, and thus he is a fuduli in the second. Branching from the mentioned principle: if a fuduli marries a man to five women in separate contracts, the husband may choose four of them and separate from the other, unlike if the man himself married five women without their permission, because his proceeding to the fifth marriage implicitly contains a revocation of the four marriages, unlike the fuduli who does not have the right to revoke explicitly or implicitly. Thus in al-Zahiriyya. Among its rulings also: a contract that is effective from one side, if it supervenes on a contract that is not effective from both sides, it overrides it; if a suspended contract supervenes on a contract that is effective from one side, it does not override; if an effective contract from one side supervenes on an effective contract from its side, it overrides. Explanation: A man appoints an agent to marry him a woman for one thousand, so the agent marries him for fifty dinars with her permission or without, then marries him for one thousand; the first is annulled. If the agent marries him for one thousand dirhams without her permission, then marries him for fifty without her permission, the first remains; if she ratifies [the first], it is valid and the second is invalid because the first is...
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage details the methods of ratification for a fuduli marriage (by words like 'I ratify' or by actions like accepting the mahr) and distinguishes between a fuduli and a wakil (agent) regarding the power to revoke the contract. The key principle: a fuduli in marriage cannot revoke his contract because he has no personal liability (uhda) to escape from, whereas an agent can revoke to fulfill the principal's intention. An exception is when the fuduli later becomes an agent; then he can revoke by deed. The passage also includes a narration from Abu Yusuf that a fuduli can revoke, but the relied-upon position is that he cannot. The section concludes with a discussion of overlapping contracts and which takes precedence.

Key Terms
عهدة (uhda): Liability or responsibility; in sale, the seller has liability for defects, so he may revoke; in marriage, no such liability.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction or revocation of a contract.
إجازة (ijaza): Ratification or approval of a contract made by a fuduli.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, why cannot a fuduli in marriage revoke his contract before ratification?
A) Because he is not the owner of the contract.
B) Because there is no liability (uhda) in marriage.
C) Because the contract is void until ratification.
D) Because he has no right to speak in marriage matters.

Key Takeaway
The fuduli in marriage cannot revoke his contract due to absence of liability, but an agent can revoke to fulfill the principal's intention; ratification can be by words or actions.

Fudhuli's Rescission in Marriage vs Sale
Ruling on fudhuli's ability to rescind marriage contract, with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on a fudhuli's right to rescind a marriage contract and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A fudhuli cannot rescind a marriage contract because marriage imposes no liability to escape, unlike sale.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the right of an unauthorized agent (fudhuli) to rescind a marriage contract before it is ratified. The source text from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq draws a sharp contrast with the law of sale, where the fudhuli can rescind by consensus. Within the Hanafi school, Imam Abu Yusuf is reported to have held a dissenting view. Understanding the legal reasoning (ta'lil) behind this distinction is essential for grasping the school's approach to contractual obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani: the fudhuli cannot rescind a marriage contract, whether verbally or by action.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variant
Note the narration from Abu Yusuf in his first opinion, which allows the fudhuli to rescind in marriage as well.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand the basis for Abu Yusuf's view: analogy (qiyas) with sale, where the fudhuli may rescind by consensus to escape liability.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority distinguishes marriage from sale: marriage does not entail the same kind of liability (ŭhdah), so there is nothing to escape from, making the analogy weak.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the fudhuli rescinds before consent, according to the majority the rescission is ineffective and the contract remains suspended; according to Abu Yusuf's narration, the rescission would be valid and the contract nullified.
The mufta bihi is the majority view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتثبت الإجازة لنكاح الفضولي بالقول والفعل؛ فمن الأول «أجزت ونحوه، وكذا نعم ما صنعت وبارك الله لنا» و«أحسنت» و«أصبت» و«طلقها» إلا إذا قال المولى لعبده كما سيأتي في بابه. ومن الثاني قبول المهر بخلاف قبول الهدية. وقولها «لا يعجبني هذا المهر ليس رداً فلها الإجازة. ومن أحكام الفضولي أنه يملك فسخ ما عقده في بعض الصور دون بعض كما ذكره أصحاب الفتاوى . قال في الظهيرية : والفضولي في باب النكاح لا يملك الرجوع قبل الإجازة والوكيل في النكاح الموقوف يملك الرجوع قولاً أو فعلاً بيانه رجل وكل رجلاً بأن يزوجه امرأة فزوجه امرأة بالغة بغير إذنها أوزوجها أبوها فلم يبلغها حتى نقض الوكيل النكاح قولاً أو فعلاً بأن يزوجه أختها صح ولو كان فضولياً، والمسألة بحالها لا يملك . وروي عن أبي يوسف في قوله الأول أن الفضولي يملك الرجوع أيضاً، والفضولي في باب البيع يملك الرجوع بالإجماع لأن الرجوع فرار عن العهدة في باب البيع بخلاف النكاح، وفي وجه الوكيل يملك الفسخ قولاً لا فعلاً بأن وكله بأن يزوجه امرأة بعينها فزوجها بغير رضاها ملك الوكيل نقضه قولاً لأنه وكيل فيه، ولا يملك نقضه فعلاً حتى لو زوجه أختها لا ينقض نكاح الأولى لأنه فضولي في نكاح الثانية، وفي وجه يملك الفسخ فعلاً لا قولاً نحو أن يوكل رجلاً بأن يزوجه فأجاز الوكيل نكاحاً باشره قبل ذلك صح استحساناً، ولا يملك نقض هذا النكاح قولاً لأنه كان فضولياً حين عقده، ويملك نقضه فعلاً بأن يزوجه أختها من غير رضاها لأنه وكيل في العقد الثاني اهـ. فحاصله أن كل عقد صدر من الفضولي في النكاح فإنه لا يملك نقضه قولاً ولا فعلاً لأنه لا عهدة عليه ليتخلص منها إلا إذا صار وكيلاً بعده فله نقضه فعلاً لضرورة امتثال ما وكل فيه، وإنما ملك الوكيل في الموقوف الفسخ مع أنه لا عهدة عليه أيضاً لتنجيز مراد الموكل فإنه لم يحصل مقصوده بالموقوف، فللوكيل الانتقال عنه إلى غيره وإنما لم يجز له الفسخ فعلاً في المسألة الثانية لأن الموكل بتزوجها معينة فحيث زوجها له انتهت وكالته فلم يملك تزويجاً آخر، ولذا كان فضولياً في الثاني.
Consent for the marriage of a fudhuli is established by speech and action. From the first: 'I consented' and the like, also 'what a good deed you have done, may Allah bless us' and 'you have done well' and 'you are correct' and 'divorce her' except if the master says to his slave as will come in its chapter. From the second: acceptance of the dower unlike acceptance of a gift. And her saying 'I do not like this dower' is not a rejection, so she has the right to consent. Among the rulings of the fudhuli is that he has the right to rescind what he contracted in some cases but not others, as mentioned by the authors of fatwas. He said in al-Zahiriyya: The fudhuli in the chapter of marriage does not have the right to return before consent, while the agent in a suspended marriage has the right to return verbally or by action. Its explanation: a man appointed an agent to marry him a woman, so he married him a mature woman without her permission, or her father married her and he did not inform her until the agent rescinded the marriage verbally or by action by marrying him her sister, it is valid even if he was a fudhuli, and the case remains that he does not have the right to rescind. It is narrated from Abu Yusuf in his first opinion that the fudhuli also has the right to return. The fudhuli in the chapter of sale has the right to return by consensus because returning is escape from liability in the chapter of sale unlike marriage. In one aspect, the agent has the right to annul verbally not by action: if he appointed him to marry him a specific woman and he married her without her consent, the agent has the right to annul it verbally because he is an agent in it, but he does not have the right to annul it by action such that if he married him her sister, the marriage of the first is not annulled because he is a fudhuli in the marriage of the second. In another aspect, he has the right to annul by action not verbally: such as if a man appointed an agent to marry him and the agent consented to a marriage that he had previously undertaken, it is valid by istihsan, and he does not have the right to annul this marriage verbally because he was a fudhuli at the time of contracting it, but he has the right to annul it by action by marrying him her sister without her consent because he is an agent in the second contract. End. The summary is that every contract issued from a fudhuli in marriage, he does not have the right to annul it verbally or by action because there is no liability on him to escape from, except if he later becomes an agent, then he has the right to annul it by action due to the necessity of complying with what he was appointed for. The agent in the suspended marriage has the right to annul even though there is no liability on him, in order to realize the intention of the principal, for his objective was not achieved by the suspended contract, so the agent may move from it to another. He was not allowed to annul by action in the second case because the principal was specific in marrying her, so when he married her for him, his agency ended, so he did not have the right to another marriage, hence he was a fudhuli in the second.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rights of a fudhuli (unauthorized agent) in a marriage contract. It first explains how consent (ijaza) for a fudhuli marriage can be given verbally (e.g., 'I consented') or by action (e.g., accepting the dower). Then it delves into the fudhuli's ability to rescind the contract. The author states the general rule: a fudhuli in marriage cannot rescind before consent, unlike in sale where he can rescind by consensus because the purpose is to escape liability. For the agent (wakil) in a suspended marriage, he can rescind verbally or by action. There is a narration from Abu Yusuf that the fudhuli can rescind in marriage as well, but the relied-upon position is that he cannot. The passage provides detailed examples comparing the fudhuli and the wakil in various scenarios. The underlying legal reason is that marriage does not impose the same kind of liability (ŭhdah) as sale.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudhuli): A person who acts without authorization, e.g., concluding a contract on behalf of another without being appointed as an agent or guardian.
رجوع (rujūʿ): Rescission or annulment of the contract by the fudhuli or agent before consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a fudhuli (unauthorized agent) rescind a marriage contract before consent?
A) Yes, by consensus.
B) No, because there is no liability to escape.
C) Yes, but only if he is also the guardian.
D) No, but he can if the marriage is not yet consummated.

Key Takeaway
A fudhuli cannot rescind a marriage contract; only an agent in a suspended marriage can, because marriage imposes no liability to escape.

The Fudhuli’s Marriage of Five Women: The Husband’s Right to Choose
Ruling on fudhuli marriage of five women and the husband's option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a fudhuli marries five women for a man and distinguish it from the case where the man himself marries them.

Core Idea
The fudhuli cannot rescind, so the man may choose four wives and separate from the fifth; but if the man himself married five, the fifth implies annulment of the previous four.

This lesson applies the key principle that a fudhuli (unauthorized agent) cannot rescind a marriage contract. In the scenario where a fudhuli marries five women on behalf of a man in separate contracts, the man is entitled to choose four (as he can only have four wives) and separate from the fifth. This is contrasted with the case where the man himself marries five women, where his action of marrying the fifth is deemed to implicitly annul the first four marriages. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi nikah rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and contracts
Note that the fudhuli (unauthorized agent) performs the marriages on behalf of the man, in separate contracts. The man is the principal who can later approve or reject.
Fudhuli means an agent acting without prior authorization.
2
State the ruling for the fudhuli case
The man may choose four of the five women and separate from the fifth. His choice validates four marriages, and the fifth is dissolved (by divorce or annulment).
The ruling is from al-Zahiriyya.
3
Contrast with the man's own action
If the man himself (without an agent) marries five women in separate contracts without their consent, his act of marrying the fifth implicitly annuls the previous four marriages. This is because his intention to have five implies he intended to annul the earlier ones.
The man's own action can imply rescission (dalalah), but the fudhuli's cannot.
4
Explain the rationale
The fudhuli lacks authority to rescind (naqd) either explicitly or by implication. Therefore, the man's selection of four does not imply rescission of the fifth; instead, he must actively separate from her.
This distinction hinges on the nature of the fudhuli's limited authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفرع على الأصل المذكور ما لو زوج فضولي رجل خمس نسوة في عقد متفرقة فللزوج أن يختار أربعاً منهن ويفارق الأخرى بخلاف ما لو تزوج الرجل خمس نسوة في عقد متفرقة بغير رضاهن لأن إقدامه على نكاح الخامسة يتضمن نقض نكاح الأربع دلالة بخلاف الفضولي لا يملك النقض لا صريحاً ولا دلالة. كذا في الظهيرية.
And derived from the mentioned principle is the case where a fudhuli married five women for a man in separate contracts; the husband may choose four of them and separate from the other, unlike if the man himself married five women in separate contracts without their consent, because his proceeding to the marriage of the fifth implies an annulment of the marriage of the four by indication, unlike the fudhuli who does not have the right to annul explicitly or by indication. Thus in al-Zahiriyya.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage applies the earlier rule that a fudhuli cannot rescind a marriage contract to a specific scenario. If a fudhuli marries five women on behalf of a man in separate contracts, the man may choose four (since he can only have four wives at a time) and separate from the fifth. This is because the fudhuli's contracts are all suspended and the man's selection of four implies rescission of the others only if the man himself married them. But since the fudhuli cannot rescind, the man's choice is valid and he divorces the fifth. If the man himself had married all five in separate contracts without their consent, his act of marrying the fifth would automatically nullify the previous four by implication (since he intended to have five, but he cannot have more than four). The fudhuli's action, however, does not carry such implication because he lacks authority to rescind.

Key Terms
فضولي (fudhūlī): An unauthorized agent who acts on behalf of another without prior permission; the contracts are suspended (mawquf).
نقض (naqḍ): Rescission or annulment of a contract, explicitly or by implication.
دلالة (dalālah): Implication; legal reasoning by indication. The man's own action of marrying the fifth implies rescission of the earlier four.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The man may choose four and the fifth is automatically annulled.
C) The man may choose four and separate from the fifth.
D) The man must keep all five if he consents.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a fudhuli cannot rescind a marriage contract; thus, when five wives are contracted, the husband may choose four and separate from the fifth, unlike the case where he contracts himself.

When a Subsequent Valid Contract Overrides an Earlier Suspended One in Nikah
Ruling on contract hierarchy in marriage via an agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule regarding the effect of a subsequent valid contract on an earlier suspended one in marriage, and apply it to examples involving an agent.

Core Idea
A contract effective from one side cancels a fully suspended one, but not vice versa.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sets out a key principle governing the interaction of marriage contracts when an agent (wakil) acts. It explains that contracts can be 'effective from one side' (nāfidh min jānib) or 'suspended' (mawqūf), and their timing matters. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for determining which contract stands when multiple contracts are performed by an agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify Contract Categories
First, understand the three categories: (1) effective from both sides (nāfidh al-jānibayn), (2) effective from one side (nāfidh min jānib), (3) not effective from both sides (ghayr nāfidh al-jānibayn) which is suspended.
Effectiveness depends on consent: if both parties consent, the contract is fully effective; if only one consents, it's effective from that side; if neither is bound, it's suspended.
2
Understand the Rule
The general rule: a contract that is effective from one side, when it supervenes upon a contract that is not effective from both sides (i.e., fully suspended), cancels the earlier contract. Conversely, if a later contract is fully suspended, it does not cancel an earlier contract that is effective from one side.
This is based on the idea that a stronger contract (more effectiveness) overrides a weaker one.
3
Apply to Example 1
Agent first marries the man to a woman for 50 dinars without her consent (suspended from both sides). Then later marries them for 1000 with her consent (effective from both sides). The first contract is annulled because the second is fully effective.
The woman's consent in the second contract makes it effective from both sides, thus overriding the suspended first contract.
4
Apply to Example 2
Agent first marries for 1000 dirhams without woman's consent (effective from man's side only). Then later marries for 50 without consent (fully suspended). The first contract remains; if the woman later consents to the first, it is valid and the second is invalidated.
The first contract is effective from the man's side, so it is stronger than the fully suspended second contract.
5
Note the Authority
The text cites Al-Zahiriyya as a source, indicating this is a well-established ruling in the Hanafi school. Recognize that such rules are part of the furu' al-fiqh of nikah.
Zahir al-Riwayah texts are the core Hanafi sources; Al-Zahiriyya likely refers to a collection of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أحكامه أيضاً أن العقد النافذ من جانب إذا طرأ على غير نافذ الجانبين يرفعه، ولو طرأ موقوف على نافذ من أحد الجانبين لا يرفعه، ولو طرأ نافذ من أحد الجانبين على نافذ جانبه يرفعه بيانه رجل وكل رجلاً بأن يزوجه امرأة بألف فزوجها إياه على خمسين ديناراً بإذنها أو بغير إذنها ثم زوجها بألف ينفسخ الأول، ولو زوجها الوكيل إياه بألف درهم بغير إذنها ثم زوجها إياه بخمسين بغير إذنها يبقي الأول، فإن إجازته جاز ويبطل الثاني لأن الأول كان نافذاً من وجه. كذا في الظهيرية أيضاً.
And among its rulings also is that a contract that is effective from one side, if it arises upon a contract that is not effective from both sides, it cancels it. And if a suspended contract arises upon a contract that is effective from one side, it does not cancel it. And if a contract effective from one side arises upon a contract effective from its side, it cancels it. Its explanation: a man appointed an agent to marry him a woman for one thousand, so he married her to him for fifty dinars with her permission or without her permission, then he married her for one thousand, the first is annulled. And if the agent married her to him for one thousand dirhams without her permission, then married her to him for fifty without her permission, the first remains; if he consents, it is valid and the second is invalidated because the first was effective from one side. Thus in al-Zahiriyya also.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents several rules about the interaction of contracts when a fudhuli or agent is involved. The key principle: a contract that is 'effective from one side' (nāfidh min jānib) can override a contract that is not effective from both sides. For example, if an agent first marries a woman to a man for a lower dower (fifty dinars) without her consent, and then later marries her to the same man for the specified dower (one thousand), the first contract (which was effective from the man's side but not from the woman's) is annulled by the second valid contract. Conversely, if the agent first performs a contract that is effective from one side (e.g., with the man's consent but not the woman's at the time) and then later does another that is suspended from both sides, the first remains and the second is invalid upon consent. This shows the hierarchy of contractual validity in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
نافذ من جانب (nāfidh min jānib): A contract that is valid and effective with respect to one of the parties (e.g., because that party consented or is the principal).
موقوف (mawqūf): A contract that is suspended, i.e., not binding on either party until consent is given (usually by the party whose consent was missing).
نافذ الجانبين (nāfidh al-jānibayn): A contract that is effective from both sides, i.e., fully valid and binding on both parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if an agent first marries a woman to a man for a lower dower without her consent, and then later marries her for the full dower with her consent, what is the status of the first contract?
A) The first contract remains valid and the second is invalid.
B) The first contract is annulled by the second.
C) Both contracts are valid and the man can choose.
D) The second contract is void because the first was earlier.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the effectiveness of a marriage contract (based on consent) determines its hierarchical priority; a more effective contract overrides a less effective earlier one.

Validity of Consent After Fudhuli's Death in Marriage vs Sale
Ruling that consent to a fudhuli marriage is valid after the fudhuli's death, unlike in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the validity of consent after a fudhuli's death in marriage versus sale and state the condition for marriage's validity.

Core Idea
Consent to a fudhuli marriage stands even after the fudhuli's death, but not for sale.

This lesson examines a key distinction between marriage and sale when a fudhuli (unauthorised intermediary) dies before the principal's consent. Understanding this distinction reveals how Hanafi fiqh treats marriage as a contract of permanence, attaching rights to the contract itself rather than the intermediary's person. You will learn the precise condition that must be met for marriage, and how sale differs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on the validity of consent (ijaza) given by the principal after the fudhuli's death. For marriage, it is valid; for sale, it is invalid.
The fudhuli is the one who contracts without authorisation.
2
Note the exception for sale
Contrast: consent after death in sale is not valid. This is the key difference highlighted by al-Zayla'i.
Sale is a commutative contract; the fudhuli's presence is considered essential.
3
Extract the condition for marriage
For marriage to be valid after the fudhuli's death, two parties must be alive: the person for whom the contract was made (al-ma'qud lahu) and one of the original contracting parties who contracts for himself.
The one contracting for himself is typically the husband or his representative.
4
Compare with sale's condition
In sale, four parties must be alive: buyer, seller, the person for whom the fudhuli acted, and the owner of the price commodity.
This shows the greater delicacy of sale.
5
Understand the underlying rationale
Marriage is a contract of permanence (a contract of 'ayn), so the right to consent is attached to the contract itself. Sale is temporary and transactional, so the fudhuli's person is more integral.
This rationale is implicit in the Hanafi approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن إجازة نكاح الفضولي صحيحة بعد موت العاقد الفضولي بخلاف إجازة بيعه بعد موته . ذكره الزيلعي في بيع الفضولي. فعلى هذا يشترط قيام المعقود له وأحد العاقدين لنفسه فقط بخلاف البيع فإنه يشترط قيام أربعة مع الثمن إن كان عرضاً .
Then know that the consent to the marriage of a fudhuli is valid after the death of the contracting fudhuli, unlike consent to his sale after his death. It is mentioned by al-Zayla'i in the sale of a fudhuli. On this basis, it is required that the person for whom the contract was made and one of the two contracting parties (the one contracting for himself only) are alive, unlike in sale where it is required that four are alive along with the price if it is a commodity.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage highlights a difference between marriage and sale concerning the fudhuli's death. In marriage, if the fudhuli dies before consent is given, the consent (ijaza) of the principal is still valid and the marriage becomes effective. In sale, however, the fudhuli's death renders the contract void and consent after death is invalid. This is because marriage is a contract of permanence and the right to consent is attached to the contract itself, not the person of the fudhuli. The text then deduces a practical condition: for marriage to be valid after the fudhuli's death, the person for whom the contract was made (the ma'qud lahu) and one of the two original contracting parties (the one acting for himself) must be alive. For sale, four parties must be alive: buyer, seller, the person for whom the fudhuli acted, and the owner of the commodity.

Key Terms
المعقود له (al-maʻqūd lahu): The person for whose benefit the fudhuli made the contract (e.g., the husband in marriage).
الفضولي (al-fudhūlī): A person who contracts on behalf of another without prior authorisation.
الإجازة (al-ijāzah): Consent or ratification given after the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Is consent (ijaza) to a fudhuli marriage valid after the fudhuli's death?
A) No, it becomes void.
B) Yes, it is valid.
C) Only if the marriage was consummated.
D) Only if the fudhuli was a guardian.

Key Takeaway
Consent to a fudhuli marriage is valid after the fudhuli's death, provided the ma'qud lahu and one original contracting party survive, unlike sale which requires four parties alive.
